<img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1048" data-file-width="648" decoding="async" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Woman-power_emblem.svg/17px-Woman-power_emblem.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Woman-power_emblem.svg/26px-Woman-power_emblem.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Woman-power_emblem.svg/34px-Woman-power_emblem.svg.png 2x" width="17"/> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Feminism" title="Portal:Feminism">Feminism portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Feminism_sidebar" title="Template:Feminism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Feminism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Feminism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Feminism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A <b>woman</b> is an adult <a href="/wiki/Female" title="Female">female</a> <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mosby_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mosby-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Venes_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Venes-2">[2]</a></sup> Prior to adulthood, a female human is referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Girl" title="Girl">girl</a> (a female <a href="/wiki/Child" title="Child">child</a> or <a href="/wiki/Adolescence" title="Adolescence">adolescent</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The plural <i>women</i> is sometimes used in certain phrases such as "<a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">women's rights</a>" to denote female humans regardless of age.
</p><p>Typically, women inherit a pair of <a href="/wiki/X_chromosome" title="X chromosome">X chromosomes</a> from their parents, and are capable of <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a> and giving <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">birth</a> from <a href="/wiki/Puberty" title="Puberty">puberty</a> until <a href="/wiki/Menopause" title="Menopause">menopause</a>. More generally, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sex_differentiation" title="Sex differentiation">sex differentiation</a> of the female fetus is governed by the lack of a present, or functioning, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SRY" title="SRY">SRY</a>-gene on either one of the respective sex chromosomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Passarge_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Passarge-4">[4]</a></sup> Female anatomy is distinguished from male anatomy by the <a href="/wiki/Female_reproductive_system" title="Female reproductive system">female reproductive system</a>, which includes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovaries" title="Ovaries">ovaries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fallopian_tube" title="Fallopian tube">fallopian tubes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uterus" title="Uterus">uterus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vagina" title="Vagina">vagina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vulva" title="Vulva">vulva</a>. The adult female <a href="/wiki/Pelvis" title="Pelvis">pelvis</a> is wider, the <a href="/wiki/Hip" title="Hip">hips</a> broader, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breasts" title="Breasts">breasts</a> larger than that of adult males. Women have significantly less facial and other body hair, have a higher body fat composition, and are on average shorter and less muscular than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Men" title="Men">men</a>.
</p><p>Throughout <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_world" title="History of the world">human history</a>, traditional <a href="/wiki/Gender_role" title="Gender role">gender roles</a> have often defined and limited women's activities and opportunities; many religious doctrines stipulate certain rules for women. With restrictions loosening during the 20th century in many societies, women have gained access to careers beyond the traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homemaker" title="Homemaker">homemaker</a>, and the ability to pursue higher education. <a href="/wiki/Violence_against_women" title="Violence against women">Violence against women</a>, whether within families or in communities, has a long history and is primarily committed by men. Some women are denied <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_rights" title="Reproductive rights">reproductive rights</a>. The movements and ideologies of <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">feminism</a> have a shared goal of achieving <a href="/wiki/Gender_equality" title="Gender equality">gender equality</a>.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trans_women" title="Trans women">Trans women</a> have a <a href="/wiki/Gender_identity" title="Gender identity">gender identity</a> that does not align with their male <a href="/wiki/Sex_assignment" title="Sex assignment">sex assignment</a> at birth, while <a href="/wiki/Intersex" title="Intersex">intersex</a> women may have sex characteristics that do not fit typical notions of female biology.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The spelling of "woman" in English has progressed over the past millennium from <i>wīfmann</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> to <i>wīmmann</i> to <i>wumman</i>, and finally, the modern spelling <i>woman</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>, <i>wīfmann</i> meant "woman" (literally "woman-person"), whereas <i>wer</i> meant "man". <i>Mann</i> had a gender-neutral meaning of "human", corresponding to Modern English "person" or "someone"; however, subsequent to the <a href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest" title="Norman Conquest">Norman Conquest</a>, <i>man</i> began to be used more in reference to "male human", and by the late 13th century it had begun to eclipse usage of the older term <i>wer</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The medial labial consonants f and m in <i>wīfmann</i> coalesced into the modern form "woman", while the initial element <i>wīf</i>, which had also meant "woman", underwent semantic narrowing to the sense of a married woman ("wife").
</p><p>It is a <a href="/wiki/Folk_etymology" title="Folk etymology">popular misconception</a> that the term "woman" is <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">etymologically</a> connected to "womb".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> "Womb" derives from the Old English word <i>wamb</i> meaning "belly, uterus"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-9">[9]</a></sup> (cognate to the modern German colloquial term "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Wamme" title="wikt:Wamme">Wamme</a>" from Old High German <i>wamba</i> for "belly, paunch, lap").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starostin_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Starostin-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kluge_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kluge-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Girl" title="Girl">girl</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgin" title="Virgin">virgin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mother" title="Mother">mother</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wife" title="Wife">wife</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daughter" title="Daughter">daughter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goodwife" title="Goodwife">goodwife</a>, <a href="/wiki/Godparent" title="Godparent">godmother</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lady" title="Lady">lady</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maid" title="Maid">maid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maiden" title="Maiden">maiden</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Widow" title="Widow">widow</a></div>
<p><b>Womanhood</b> is the period in a human female's life after she has passed through childhood, puberty, and adolescence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="Making a medical claim, should probably have a MEDRS to support it. (July 2022)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Different countries have different laws, but age 18 is frequently considered the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_majority" title="Age of majority">age of majority</a> (the age at which a person is legally considered an adult).
</p><p>The word <i>woman</i> can be used generally, to mean any female human, or specifically, to mean an adult female human as contrasted with <i>girl</i>. The word <i>girl</i> originally meant "young person of either sex" in English;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> it was only around the beginning of the 16th century that it came to mean specifically a <i>female</i> child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The term <i>girl</i> is sometimes used colloquially to refer to a young or unmarried woman; however, during the early 1970s, feminists challenged such use because the use of the word to refer to a fully grown woman may cause offence. In particular, previously common terms such as <i>office girl</i> are no longer widely used. Conversely, in certain cultures which link family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honor" title="Honor">honor</a> with female <a href="/wiki/Virginity" title="Virginity">virginity</a>, the word <i>girl</i> (or its equivalent in other languages) is still used to refer to a never-married woman; in this sense it is used in a fashion roughly analogous to the more-or-less obsolete English <i>maid</i> or <i>maiden</i>.
</p><p>There are various words used to refer to the quality of being a woman. The term "womanhood" merely means the state of being a woman; "femininity" is used to refer to a set of typical female qualities associated with a certain attitude to <a href="/wiki/Gender_role" title="Gender role">gender roles</a>; "womanliness" is like "femininity", but is usually associated with a different view of gender roles. "Distaff" is an archaic adjective derived from women's conventional role as a spinner, now used only as a deliberate <a href="/wiki/Archaism" title="Archaism">archaism</a>.
</p><p>Menarche, the onset of <a href="/wiki/Menstruation" title="Menstruation">menstruation</a>, occurs on average at age 12–13. Many cultures have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rites_of_passage" title="Rites of passage">rites of passage</a> to symbolize a girl's <a href="/wiki/Coming_of_age" title="Coming of age">coming of age</a>, such as confirmation in some branches of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bat_mitzvah" title="Bat mitzvah">bat mitzvah</a> in <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, or a custom of a special celebration for a certain <a href="/wiki/Birthday" title="Birthday">birthday</a> (generally between 12 and 21), like the <a href="/wiki/Quincea%C3%B1era" title="Quinceañera">quinceañera</a> of Latin America.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trans_women" title="Trans women">Trans women</a> had a male <a href="/wiki/Sex_assignment" title="Sex assignment">sex assignment</a> at birth that does not align with their <a href="/wiki/Gender_identity" title="Gender identity">gender identity</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Intersex" title="Intersex">intersex</a> women may have sex characteristics that do not fit typical notions of female biology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology">Biology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sky_spectral_karyotype.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="342" data-file-width="432" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Sky_spectral_karyotype.png/220px-Sky_spectral_karyotype.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Sky_spectral_karyotype.png/330px-Sky_spectral_karyotype.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/35/Sky_spectral_karyotype.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sky_spectral_karyotype.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectral_karyotype" title="Spectral karyotype">Spectral karyotype</a> of a human female</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_characteristics">Genetic characteristics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sexual_differentiation_in_humans" title="Sexual differentiation in humans">Sexual differentiation in humans</a></div>
<p>Normally cells from females contain two X chromosomes, and cells from males contain an X and a Y chromosome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hake_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hake-18">[18]</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/Human_fertilization" title="Human fertilization">early fetal development</a> embryo morphology of both sexes is similar until about week 6 or 7 when gonads differentiate into testes in males due to the action of the Y chromosome. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sex_differentiation" title="Sex differentiation">Sex differentiation</a> proceeds in females in a way that is independent of gonadal hormones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Because humans inherit <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> only from the mother's ovum, <a href="/wiki/Genetic_genealogy" title="Genetic genealogy">genealogical</a> researchers can trace <a href="/wiki/Matrilineality" title="Matrilineality">maternal lineage</a> far back in time.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anterior_view_of_human_female_and_male,_with_labels_2.png"><img alt="Photograph of an adult female human, with an adult male for comparison. Note that the pubic hair of both models is removed." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="867" data-file-width="818" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Anterior_view_of_human_female_and_male%2C_with_labels_2.png/220px-Anterior_view_of_human_female_and_male%2C_with_labels_2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Anterior_view_of_human_female_and_male%2C_with_labels_2.png/330px-Anterior_view_of_human_female_and_male%2C_with_labels_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Anterior_view_of_human_female_and_male%2C_with_labels_2.png/440px-Anterior_view_of_human_female_and_male%2C_with_labels_2.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anterior_view_of_human_female_and_male,_with_labels_2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photograph of an adult female human, with an adult male for comparison. Note that the <a href="/wiki/Pubic_hair" title="Pubic hair">pubic hair</a> of both models is removed.</div></div></div>
<h3><span id="Hormonal_characteristics.2C_menstruation_and_menopause"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hormonal_characteristics,_menstruation_and_menopause">Hormonal characteristics, menstruation and menopause</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Menstrual_cycle" title="Menstrual cycle">Menstrual cycle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Menstruation" title="Menstruation">Menstruation</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Puberty" title="Puberty">Puberty</a> in females triggers changes in the <a href="/wiki/Human_body" title="Human body">body</a> that enable <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">sexual reproduction</a> via <a href="/wiki/Fertilisation" title="Fertilisation">fertilization</a>. In response to chemical signals from the <a href="/wiki/Pituitary_gland" title="Pituitary gland">pituitary gland</a>, ovaries secrete hormones that stimulate maturation of the body, including increased height and weight, body hair growth, breast development and <a href="/wiki/Menarche" title="Menarche">menarche</a> (the onset of menstruation) which generally occurs between ages 12–13.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U.S._menarche_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-U.S._menarche-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canadian_menarche_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canadian_menarche-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UK_menarche_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-UK_menarche-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most girls go through <a href="/wiki/Menarche" title="Menarche">menarche</a> and are then able to become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pregnant" title="Pregnant">pregnant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">bear children</a>. This generally requires internal fertilization of her eggs with the sperm of a man through <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">sexual intercourse</a>, though <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_technology" title="Reproductive technology">reproductive technology</a> offers alternatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Humans are similar to other large mammals in that they usually birth one offspring per pregnancy, but are unusual in being <a href="/wiki/Precociality_and_altriciality" title="Precociality and altriciality">altricial</a> compared to most other large mammals, meaning young are <a href="/wiki/Child_development" title="Child development">undeveloped</a> at time of birth and require the aid of their parents or guardians to fully mature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Sometimes humans have <a href="/wiki/Multiple_birth" title="Multiple birth">multiple births</a>, most commonly <a href="/wiki/Twin" title="Twin">twins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Typically between 49 and 52 years of age a woman reaches <a href="/wiki/Menopause" title="Menopause">menopause</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013Def_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013Def-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PubMed2013_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-PubMed2013-28">[28]</a></sup> (also known as the climacteric), which is the time in women's lives when menstrual periods stop permanently, and they are no longer able to bear children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tak2015_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tak2015-29">[29]</a></sup> Unlike most other mammals, the human lifespan usually extends many years beyond menopause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Many women become <a href="/wiki/Grandparent" title="Grandparent">grandmothers</a> and contribute to the care of grandchildren and other family members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Many biologists believe that the extended human lifespan is evolutionarily driven by <a href="/wiki/Kin_selection" title="Kin selection">kin selection</a>, though other theories have also been proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scheme_female_reproductive_system-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="334" data-file-width="567" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Scheme_female_reproductive_system-en.svg/220px-Scheme_female_reproductive_system-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Scheme_female_reproductive_system-en.svg/330px-Scheme_female_reproductive_system-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Scheme_female_reproductive_system-en.svg/440px-Scheme_female_reproductive_system-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scheme_female_reproductive_system-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_female_reproductive_system" title="Human female reproductive system">human female reproductive system</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphological_and_physiological_characteristics">Morphological and physiological characteristics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sex_differences_in_human_physiology" title="Sex differences in human physiology">Sex differences in human physiology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Secondary_sex_characteristic" title="Secondary sex characteristic">Secondary sex characteristic</a></div>
<p>In terms of <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, the female <a href="/wiki/Sex_organ" title="Sex organ">sex organs</a> are involved in the reproductive system, whereas the <a href="/wiki/Secondary_sex_characteristic" title="Secondary sex characteristic">secondary sex characteristics</a> are involved in <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding" title="Breastfeeding">breastfeeding</a> children and attracting a mate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buss_2019_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buss_2019-36">[36]</a></sup> Humans are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Placental_mammals" title="Placental mammals">placental mammals</a>, which means the mother carries the fetus in the uterus and the placenta facilitates the exchange of nutrients and waste between the mother and fetus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovaries" title="Ovaries">ovaries</a>, in addition to their regulatory function of producing hormones, produce female gametes called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovum" title="Ovum">ova</a> which, when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilization" title="Fertilization">fertilized</a> by male gametes (<a href="/wiki/Spermatozoon" title="Spermatozoon">sperm</a>), form new genetic individuals. The <a href="/wiki/Uterus" title="Uterus">uterus</a> is an organ with tissue to protect and nurture the developing fetus and muscle to expel it when giving birth. The <a href="/wiki/Vagina" title="Vagina">vagina</a> is used in copulation and birthing, although the term <i>vagina</i> is often colloquially and incorrectly used in the English language for the <a href="/wiki/Vulva" title="Vulva">vulva</a> (or external female genitalia),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirkpatrick_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kirkpatrick-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sáenz-Herrero_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sáenz-Herrero-40">[40]</a></sup> which consists of (in addition to the vaginal opening) the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labia_(genitalia)" title="Labia (genitalia)">labia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Clitoris" title="Clitoris">clitoris</a>, and the female <a href="/wiki/Urethra" title="Urethra">urethra</a>. The mammary glands are hypothesized to have evolved from apocrine-like glands to produce milk, a nutritious secretion that is the most distinctive characteristic of mammals, along with live birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> In mature women, the breast is generally more prominent than in most other mammals; this prominence, not necessary for milk production, is thought to be at least partially the result of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_selection" title="Sexual selection">sexual selection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buss_2019_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Buss_2019-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pregnancy_26_weeks_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1203" decoding="async" height="375" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Pregnancy_26_weeks_1.jpg/220px-Pregnancy_26_weeks_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Pregnancy_26_weeks_1.jpg/330px-Pregnancy_26_weeks_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Pregnancy_26_weeks_1.jpg/440px-Pregnancy_26_weeks_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pregnancy_26_weeks_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pregnant woman</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_distribution_and_life_expectancy">Gender distribution and life expectancy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy#Sex_differences" title="Life expectancy">Life expectancy § Sex differences</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:11-stages-womanhood-1840s.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="892" data-file-width="1314" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/11-stages-womanhood-1840s.jpg/220px-11-stages-womanhood-1840s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/11-stages-womanhood-1840s.jpg/330px-11-stages-womanhood-1840s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/11-stages-womanhood-1840s.jpg/440px-11-stages-womanhood-1840s.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:11-stages-womanhood-1840s.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A woman depicted at different ages</div></div></div>
<p>Although fewer females than males are born (the ratio is around 1:1.05), newborn girls are more likely to reach their first birthday than are boys and women typically have a longer life expectancy of six to eight years, although in some areas gender-based discrimination against women has lowered female life expectancy to lower or equal to that of males. Out of the total human population in 2015, there were 101.8 men for every 100 women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The life expectancy differences are partly due to inherent biological advantages, but they also reflect behavioral differences between men and women. The gap is narrowing to some extent in some developed countries, possibly due to increased smoking among women and declining rates of cardiovascular disease among men. The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) writes that it is "important to note that the extra years of life for women are not always lived in good health."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_health" title="Women's health">Women's health</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reproductive_health" title="Reproductive health">Reproductive health</a></div>
<p>Factors that specifically affect the health of women vs. <a href="/wiki/Men%27s_health" title="Men's health">men</a> are most evident in those related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reproductive_health" title="Reproductive health">reproduction</a>, but <a href="/wiki/Sex_differences_in_medicine" title="Sex differences in medicine">sex differences</a> have been identified from the molecular to the behavioral scale. Some of these differences are subtle and difficult to explain, partly due to the fact that it is difficult to separate the health effects of inherent biological factors from the effects of the surrounding environment they exist in. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sex_chromosomes" title="Sex chromosomes">Sex chromosomes</a> and hormones, as well as sex-specific lifestyles, metabolism, immune system function, and sensitivity to environmental factors are believed to contribute to sex differences in health at the levels of physiology, perception, and cognition. Women can have distinct responses to drugs and thresholds for diagnostic parameters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="Book has 288 pages, 6 chapters, and 4 appendices (July 2022)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Some diseases primarily affect or are exclusively found in women, such as <a href="/wiki/Lupus_erythematosus" title="Lupus erythematosus">lupus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breast_cancer" title="Breast cancer">breast cancer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cervical_cancer" title="Cervical cancer">cervical cancer</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Ovarian_cancer" title="Ovarian cancer">ovarian cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The medical practice dealing with female reproduction and reproductive organs is called <a href="/wiki/Gynaecology" title="Gynaecology">gynaecology</a> ("science of women").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="Citation is to a book review. Original book is available to cite. (July 2022)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maternal_mortality">Maternal mortality</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kabala_mother.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2267" data-file-width="2828" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Kabala_mother.jpg/260px-Kabala_mother.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Kabala_mother.jpg/390px-Kabala_mother.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Kabala_mother.jpg/520px-Kabala_mother.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kabala_mother.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leonean</a> mother <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding" title="Breastfeeding">nursing</a> an infant.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maternal_Mortality" title="Maternal Mortality">Maternal Mortality</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maternal_mortality" title="Maternal mortality">Maternal mortality</a> or maternal death is defined by WHO as "the death of a woman while pregnant or within 42 days of termination of pregnancy, irrespective of the duration and site of the pregnancy, from any cause related to or aggravated by the pregnancy or its management but not from accidental or incidental causes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> In 2008, noting that each year more than 500,000 women die of complications of pregnancy and childbirth and at least seven million experience serious health problems while 50 million more have adverse health consequences after childbirth, the World Health Organization urged midwife training to strengthen maternal and newborn health services. To support the upgrading of midwifery skills the WHO established a midwife training program, Action for Safe Motherhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2008Ed_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2008Ed-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, 94% of maternal deaths occur in low and lower middle-income countries. Approximately 86% of maternal deaths occur in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, with sub-Saharan Africa accounting for around 66% and Southern Asia accounting for around 20%. The main causes of maternal mortality include <a href="/wiki/Pre-eclampsia" title="Pre-eclampsia">pre-eclampsia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eclampsia" title="Eclampsia">eclampsia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unsafe_abortion" title="Unsafe abortion">unsafe abortion</a>, pregnancy complications from <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a>, and severe bleeding and infections following childbirth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Most European countries, Australia, Japan, and Singapore are very safe in regard to childbirth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Synthesis_of_published_material" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="Citation is to a list of statistics on maternal mortality rate. As a list of data, it does not make the claim it is being used to support. (July 2022)">improper synthesis</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="Citation is to a list of statistics that is currently over 12 years old. See also the synthesis inline tag. (July 2022)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In 1990, the US ranked 12th of the 14 developed countries that were analyzed and since that time the death rates of every country have steadily improved while the US rate has spiked dramatically. While the others that were analyzed in 1990 show a 2017 death rate of fewer than 10 deaths per every 100,000 live births, the U.S. rate rose to 26.4. Furthermore, for every one of the 700 to 900 women who die in the U.S. each year during pregnancy or childbirth, 70 experience significant complications, totaling more than one percent of all births.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproductive_rights_and_freedom">Reproductive rights and freedom</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sterilization_states.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="401" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Sterilization_states.jpg/220px-Sterilization_states.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Sterilization_states.jpg/330px-Sterilization_states.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Sterilization_states.jpg/440px-Sterilization_states.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sterilization_states.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A poster from a 1921 <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">eugenics</a> conference displays the U.S. states that had implemented sterilization legislation.</div></div></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_rights" title="Reproductive rights">Reproductive rights</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Reproductive_rights" title="Reproductive rights">Reproductive rights</a> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_right" title="Legal right">legal rights</a> and freedoms relating to <a href="/wiki/Human_reproduction" title="Human reproduction">reproduction</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reproductive_health" title="Reproductive health">reproductive health</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Federation_of_Gynecology_and_Obstetrics" title="International Federation of Gynecology and Obstetrics">International Federation of Gynecology and Obstetrics</a> has stated that:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>... the human rights of women include their right to have control over and decide freely and responsibly on matters related to their sexuality, including sexual and reproductive health, free of coercion, discrimination and violence. Equal relationships between women and men in matters of sexual relations and reproduction, including full respect for the integrity of the person, require mutual respect, consent and shared responsibility for sexual behavior and its consequences.</dd></dl>
<p>The World Health Organization reports that based on data from 2010 to 2014, 56 million induced abortions occurred worldwide each year (25% of all pregnancies). Of those, about 25 million were considered as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unsafe_abortions" title="Unsafe abortions">unsafe</a>. The WHO reports that in developed regions about 30 women die for every 100,000 unsafe abortions and that number rises to 220 deaths per 100,000 unsafe abortions in developing regions and 520 deaths per 100,000 unsafe abortions in sub-Saharan Africa. The WHO ascribes these deaths to:
</p>
<ul><li>restrictive laws</li>
<li>poor availability of services</li>
<li>high cost</li>
<li>stigma</li>
<li>conscientious objection of health-care providers</li>
<li>unnecessary requirements, such as mandatory waiting periods, mandatory counselling, provision of misleading information, third-party authorization, and medically unnecessary tests that delay care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture_and_gender_roles">Culture and gender roles</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gender_role" title="Gender role">Gender role</a></div>
<p>In recent history, gender roles have changed greatly. At some earlier points in history, children's occupational aspirations starting at a young age differed according to gender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Traditionally, <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle class</a> women were involved in domestic tasks emphasizing child care. For poorer women, especially <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working class</a> women, although this often remained an ideal,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="Statement needs to be more specific about the content to which it refers. (February 2007)">specify</span></a></i>]</sup> economic necessity compelled them to seek employment outside the home. Many of the occupations that were available to them were lower in pay than those available to men.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>As changes in the labor market for women came about, availability of employment changed from only "dirty", long hour factory jobs to "cleaner", more respectable office jobs where more education was demanded. Women's participation in the U.S. labor force rose from 6% in 1900 to 23% in 1923. These shifts in the labor force led to changes in the attitudes towards women at work, allowing for the revolution which resulted in women becoming career and education oriented.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In the 1970s, many female academics, including scientists, avoided having children. Throughout the 1980s, institutions tried to equalize conditions for men and women in the workplace. Even so, the inequalities at home hampered women's opportunities: professional women were still generally considered responsible for domestic labor and child care, which limited the time and energy they could devote to their careers. Until the early 20th century, U.S. women's colleges required their women faculty members to remain single, on the grounds that a woman could not carry on two full-time professions at once. According to Schiebinger, "Being a scientist and a wife and a mother is a burden in society that expects women more often than men to put family ahead of career." (p. 93).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Movements advocate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equality_of_opportunity" title="Equality of opportunity">equality of opportunity</a> for both sexes and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_rights" title="Civil rights">equal rights</a> irrespective of gender. Through a combination of <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economic</a> changes and the efforts of the <a href="/wiki/Feminist_movement" title="Feminist movement">feminist movement</a>, in recent decades women in many societies have gained access to careers beyond the traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homemaker" title="Homemaker">homemaker</a>. Despite these advances, modern women in Western society still face challenges in the workplace as well as with the topics of education, violence, health care, politics, and motherhood, and others. <a href="/wiki/Sexism" title="Sexism">Sexism</a> can be a main concern and barrier for women almost anywhere, though its forms, perception, and gravity vary between societies and social classes. There has been an increase in the endorsement of egalitarian gender roles in the home by both women and men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (August 2019)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Although a greater number of women are seeking higher education, their salaries are often less than those of men. CBS News said in 2005 that in the United States women who are ages 30 to 44 and hold a university degree make 62% of what similarly qualified men do, a lower rate than in all but three of the 19 countries for which numbers are available. Some Western nations with greater inequality in pay are Germany, New Zealand and Switzerland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Violence_against_women">Violence against women</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Campaign_road_sign_against_female_genital_mutilation_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="651" data-file-width="957" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Campaign_road_sign_against_female_genital_mutilation_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Campaign_road_sign_against_female_genital_mutilation_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Campaign_road_sign_against_female_genital_mutilation_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Campaign_road_sign_against_female_genital_mutilation_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Campaign_road_sign_against_female_genital_mutilation_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Campaign_road_sign_against_female_genital_mutilation_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Campaign_road_sign_against_female_genital_mutilation_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A campaign against <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a> – a road sign near <a href="/wiki/Kapchorwa" title="Kapchorwa">Kapchorwa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Violence_against_women" title="Violence against women">Violence against women</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">UN</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Declaration_on_the_Elimination_of_Violence_against_Women" title="Declaration on the Elimination of Violence against Women">Declaration on the Elimination of Violence against Women</a> defines "violence against women" as:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>any act of gender-based violence that results in, or is likely to result in, physical, sexual or mental harm or suffering to women, including threats of such acts, coercion or arbitrary deprivation of liberty, whether occurring in public or in private life.</p></blockquote>
<p>It identifies three forms of such violence: that which occurs <i>in the family</i>, that which occurs <i>within the general community</i>, and that which is perpetrated or condoned <i>by the State</i>. It also states that "violence against women is a manifestation of historically unequal power relations between men and women".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Violence against women remains a widespread problem, fueled, especially outside the West, by patriarchal social values, lack of adequate laws, and lack of enforcement of existing laws. Social norms that exist in many parts of the world hinder progress towards protecting women from violence. For example, according to surveys by <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a>, the percentage of women aged 15–49 who think that a husband is justified in hitting or beating his wife under certain circumstances is as high as 90% in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, 87% in <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>, 86% in <a href="/wiki/Guinea" title="Guinea">Guinea</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timor-Leste" title="Timor-Leste">Timor-Leste</a>, 81% in <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>, and 80% in the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> A 2010 survey conducted by the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> found that <a href="/wiki/Stoning" title="Stoning">stoning</a> as a punishment for <a href="/wiki/Adultery" title="Adultery">adultery</a> was supported by 82% of respondents in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, 70% in <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, 56% <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, and 42% in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Specific forms of violence that affect women include <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sex_trafficking" title="Sex trafficking">sex trafficking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forced_prostitution" title="Forced prostitution">forced prostitution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forced_marriage" title="Forced marriage">forced marriage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rape" title="Rape">rape</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sexual_harassment" title="Sexual harassment">sexual harassment</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honor_killings" title="Honor killings">honor killings</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acid_throwing" title="Acid throwing">acid throwing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dowry_death" title="Dowry death">dowry related violence</a>. Governments can be complicit in violence against women, such as when stoning is used as a legal punishment, mostly for women accused of adultery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>There have also been many forms of violence against women which have been prevalent historically, notably the <a href="/wiki/Witch-hunt" title="Witch-hunt">burning of witches</a>, the sacrifice of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Widows" title="Widows">widows</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Sati_(practice)" title="Sati (practice)">sati</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Foot_binding" title="Foot binding">foot binding</a>. The prosecution of women accused of <a href="/wiki/Witchcraft" title="Witchcraft">witchcraft</a> has a long tradition; for example, during the early modern period (between the 15th and 18th centuries), <a href="/wiki/Witch_trials_in_the_early_modern_period" title="Witch trials in the early modern period">witch trials</a> were common in Europe and in the European colonies in North America. Today, there remain regions of the world (such as parts of Sub-Saharan Africa, rural North India, and Papua New Guinea) where belief in witchcraft is held by many people, and women accused of being witches are subjected to serious violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> In addition, there are also countries which have criminal legislation against the practice of witchcraft. In <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, witchcraft remains a crime <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punishable_by_death" title="Punishable by death">punishable by death</a>, and in 2011 the country beheaded a woman for 'witchcraft and sorcery'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is also the case that certain forms of violence against women have been recognized as criminal offenses only during recent decades, and are not universally prohibited, in that many countries continue to allow them. This is especially the case with <a href="/wiki/Marital_rape" title="Marital rape">marital rape</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> In the Western World, there has been a trend towards ensuring <a href="/wiki/Gender_equality" title="Gender equality">gender equality</a> within <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">marriage</a> and prosecuting <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic violence</a>, but in many parts of the world women still lose significant legal rights when entering a marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sexual violence against women greatly increases during times of <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">war</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armed_conflict" title="Armed conflict">armed conflict</a>, during <a href="/wiki/Military_occupation" title="Military occupation">military occupation</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_conflict" title="Ethnic conflict">ethnic conflicts</a>; most often in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_rape" title="War rape">war rape</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sexual_slavery" title="Sexual slavery">sexual slavery</a>. Contemporary examples of sexual violence during war include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rape_during_the_Armenian_Genocide" title="Rape during the Armenian Genocide">rape during the Armenian Genocide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rape_during_the_Bangladesh_Liberation_War" title="Rape during the Bangladesh Liberation War">rape during the Bangladesh Liberation War</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rape_in_the_Bosnian_War" title="Rape in the Bosnian War">rape in the Bosnian War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rape_during_the_Rwandan_genocide" title="Rape during the Rwandan genocide">rape during the Rwandan genocide</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_rape#Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="War rape">rape during Second Congo War</a>. In Colombia, the armed conflict has also resulted in increased sexual violence against women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The most recent case was the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_jihad" title="Sexual jihad">sexual jihad</a> done by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISIL" title="ISIL">ISIL</a> where 5000–7000 Yazidi and Christian girls and children were sold into sexual slavery during the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_violence_in_the_Iraqi_insurgency" title="Sexual violence in the Iraqi insurgency">genocide and rape of Yazidi and Christian women</a>, some of which jumped to their death from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Sinjar" title="Mount Sinjar">Mount Sinjar</a>, as described in a witness statement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rudaw-26082014_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-rudaw-26082014-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Laws and policies on violence against women vary by jurisdiction. In the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, sexual harassment and human trafficking are subject to <a href="/wiki/Directive_(European_Union)" title="Directive (European Union)">directives</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<p>The earliest women whose names are known include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Neithhotep" title="Neithhotep">Neithhotep</a> (c. 3200 BCE), the wife of <a href="/wiki/Narmer" title="Narmer">Narmer</a> and the first queen of ancient Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Merneith" title="Merneith">Merneith</a> (c. 3000 BCE), <a href="/wiki/Queen_consort" title="Queen consort">consort</a> and regent of ancient Egypt during the <a href="/wiki/First_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="First Dynasty of Egypt">first dynasty</a>. She may have been ruler of Egypt in her own right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.co.uk_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.co.uk-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Peseshet" title="Peseshet">Peseshet</a> (c. 2600 BCE), a <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Puabi" title="Puabi">Puabi</a> (c. 2600 BCE), or Shubad – queen of <a href="/wiki/Ur" title="Ur">Ur</a> whose tomb was discovered with many expensive artifacts. Other known pre-Sargonic queens of Ur (royal wives) include Ashusikildigir, Ninbanda, and Gansamannu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kubaba" title="Kubaba">Kugbau</a> (<i>circa</i> 2,500 BCE), a taverness from <a href="/wiki/Kish_(Sumer)" title="Kish (Sumer)">Kish</a> chosen by the <a href="/wiki/Nippur" title="Nippur">Nippur</a> priesthood to become hegemonic ruler of <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a>, and in later ages deified as "Kubaba".</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tashlultum" title="Tashlultum">Tashlultum</a> (c. 2400 BCE), <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian</a> queen, wife of <a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">Sargon of Akkad</a> and mother of Enheduanna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baranamtarra" title="Baranamtarra">Baranamtarra</a> (c. 2384 BCE), prominent and influential queen of <a href="/wiki/Lugalanda" title="Lugalanda">Lugalanda</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lagash" title="Lagash">Lagash</a>. Other known pre-Sargonic queens of the first Lagash dynasty include Menbara-abzu, Ashume'eren, Ninkhilisug, Dimtur, and Shagshag, and the names of several princesses are also known.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Enheduanna" title="Enheduanna">Enheduanna</a> (c. 2285 BCE),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Ordination_of_women#Sumer_and_Akkad" title="Ordination of women">high priestess</a> of the temple of the <a href="/wiki/Sin_(mythology)" title="Sin (mythology)">Moon God</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> city-state of <a href="/wiki/Ur" title="Ur">Ur</a> and possibly the first known poet and first named author of either gender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Shibtu" title="Shibtu">Shibtu</a> (c. 1775 BCE), king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zimrilim" title="Zimrilim">Zimrilim</a>'s consort and queen of the Syrian city-state of <a href="/wiki/Mari,_Syria" title="Mari, Syria">Mari</a>. During her husband's absence, she ruled as regent of Mari and enjoyed extensive administrative powers as queen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span id="Clothing.2C_fashion_and_dress_codes"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Clothing,_fashion_and_dress_codes">Clothing, fashion and dress codes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:323px;max-width:323px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:167px;max-width:167px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zohre_Esmaeli_1.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2379" data-file-width="1784" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Zohre_Esmaeli_1.jpg/165px-Zohre_Esmaeli_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Zohre_Esmaeli_1.jpg/248px-Zohre_Esmaeli_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Zohre_Esmaeli_1.jpg/330px-Zohre_Esmaeli_1.jpg 2x" width="165"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KIMONO_GORLS_IN_TOKYO_A.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="5472" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/KIMONO_GORLS_IN_TOKYO_A.jpg/150px-KIMONO_GORLS_IN_TOKYO_A.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/KIMONO_GORLS_IN_TOKYO_A.jpg/225px-KIMONO_GORLS_IN_TOKYO_A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/KIMONO_GORLS_IN_TOKYO_A.jpg/300px-KIMONO_GORLS_IN_TOKYO_A.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Women's traditional clothing varies highly in different cultures. From left to right: Afghan model wearing traditional Afghan dress and Japanese women wearing <a href="/wiki/Kimono" title="Kimono">kimono</a>.</div></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fashion" title="Fashion">Fashion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Modesty" title="Modesty">Modesty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clothing" title="Clothing">Clothing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hijab_by_country" title="Hijab by country">Hijab by country</a></div>
<p>Women in different parts of the world dress in different ways, with their choices of clothing being influenced by local culture, religious tenets, traditions, social norms, and fashion trends, amongst other factors. Different societies have different ideas about <a href="/wiki/Modesty" title="Modesty">modesty</a>. However, in many jurisdictions, women's choices in regard to dress are not always free, with laws limiting what they may or may not wear. This is especially the case in regard to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islam_and_clothing" title="Islam and clothing">Islamic dress</a>. While certain jurisdictions legally mandate such clothing (the wearing of the headscarf), other countries forbid or restrict the wearing of certain <a href="/wiki/Hijab" title="Hijab">hijab</a> attire (such as <a href="/wiki/Burqa" title="Burqa">burqa</a>/covering the face) in public places (one such country is <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> – see <a href="/wiki/French_ban_on_face_covering" title="French ban on face covering">French ban on face covering</a>). These laws – both those mandating and those prohibiting certain articles of dress – are highly controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fertility_and_family_life">Fertility and family life</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mother" title="Mother">Mother</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bhutan_(63774069).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="928" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Bhutan_%2863774069%29.jpg/220px-Bhutan_%2863774069%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Bhutan_%2863774069%29.jpg/330px-Bhutan_%2863774069%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Bhutan_%2863774069%29.jpg/440px-Bhutan_%2863774069%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bhutan_(63774069).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mother and child, in <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="566" data-file-width="1104" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg/300px-Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg/450px-Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg/600px-Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of countries by fertility rate (2020), according to the <a href="/wiki/Population_Reference_Bureau" title="Population Reference Bureau">Population Reference Bureau</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Percentage_of_birth_to_unmarried_women,_selected_countries,_1980_and_2007.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="571" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Percentage_of_birth_to_unmarried_women%2C_selected_countries%2C_1980_and_2007.png/440px-Percentage_of_birth_to_unmarried_women%2C_selected_countries%2C_1980_and_2007.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Percentage_of_birth_to_unmarried_women%2C_selected_countries%2C_1980_and_2007.png/660px-Percentage_of_birth_to_unmarried_women%2C_selected_countries%2C_1980_and_2007.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Percentage_of_birth_to_unmarried_women%2C_selected_countries%2C_1980_and_2007.png/880px-Percentage_of_birth_to_unmarried_women%2C_selected_countries%2C_1980_and_2007.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Percentage_of_birth_to_unmarried_women,_selected_countries,_1980_and_2007.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Percentage of births to unmarried women, selected countries, 1980 and 2007<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-non_mar1_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-non_mar1-92">[92]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">total fertility rate</a> (TFR) – the average number of children born to a woman over her lifetime – differs significantly between different regions of the world. In 2016, the highest estimated TFR was in <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> (6.62 children born per woman) and the lowest in <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> (0.82 children/woman).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> While most Sub-Saharan African countries have a high TFR, which creates problems due to lack of resources and contributes to <a href="/wiki/Overpopulation" title="Overpopulation">overpopulation</a>, most Western countries currently experience a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sub_replacement_fertility_rate" title="Sub replacement fertility rate">sub replacement fertility rate</a> which may lead to <a href="/wiki/Population_ageing" title="Population ageing">population ageing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Population_decline" title="Population decline">population decline</a>.
</p><p>In many parts of the world, there has been a change in family structure over the past few decades. For instance, in the West, there has been a trend of moving away from living arrangements that include the <a href="/wiki/Extended_family" title="Extended family">extended family</a> to those which only consist of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear family</a>. There has also been a trend to move from marital fertility to non-marital fertility. Children born outside marriage may be born to <a href="/wiki/Cohabitation" title="Cohabitation">cohabiting couples</a> or to <a href="/wiki/Single_parent" title="Single parent">single women</a>. While births outside marriage are common and fully accepted in some parts of the world, in other places they are highly stigmatized, with unmarried mothers facing ostracism, including violence from family members, and in extreme cases even <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honor_killings" title="Honor killings">honor killings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> In addition, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sex_outside_marriage" title="Sex outside marriage">sex outside marriage</a> remains illegal in many countries (such as Saudi Arabia, Pakistan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Afghanistan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-travel.state.gov_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-travel.state.gov-98">[98]</a></sup> Iran,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-travel.state.gov_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-travel.state.gov-98">[98]</a></sup> Kuwait,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Maldives,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Morocco,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Oman,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Mauritania,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> United Arab Emirates,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Sudan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> and Yemen<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>).
</p><p>The social role of the <a href="/wiki/Mother" title="Mother">mother</a> differs between cultures. In many parts of the world, women with dependent children are expected to stay at home and dedicate all their energy to child raising, while in other places mothers most often return to paid work (see <a href="/wiki/Working_parent" title="Working parent">working mother</a> and <a href="/wiki/Housewife" title="Housewife">stay-at-home mother</a>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Christianity" title="Women in Christianity">Women in Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Judaism" title="Women in Judaism">Women in Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Islam" title="Women in Islam">Women in Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Zoroastrianism" title="Women in Zoroastrianism">Women in Zoroastrianism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Women_in_Mormonism" title="Women in Mormonism">Women in Mormonism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Hinduism" title="Women in Hinduism">Women in Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Sikhism" title="Women in Sikhism">Women in Sikhism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Buddhism" title="Women in Buddhism">Women in Buddhism</a></div>
<p>Particular religious doctrines have specific stipulations relating to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gender_roles" title="Gender roles">gender roles</a>, social and private interaction between the sexes, appropriate dressing attire for women, and various other issues affecting women and their position in society. In many countries, these religious teachings influence the <a href="/wiki/Criminal_law" title="Criminal law">criminal law</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/Family_law" title="Family law">family law</a> of those jurisdictions (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharia_law" title="Sharia law">Sharia law</a>, for example). The relation between religion, law and gender equality has been discussed by international organizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Female_education" title="Female education">Female education</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bolivia_la_paz_literacy_LOC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="496" data-file-width="351" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Bolivia_la_paz_literacy_LOC.jpg/180px-Bolivia_la_paz_literacy_LOC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Bolivia_la_paz_literacy_LOC.jpg/270px-Bolivia_la_paz_literacy_LOC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7c/Bolivia_la_paz_literacy_LOC.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bolivia_la_paz_literacy_LOC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Women attending an adult literacy class in the <a href="/wiki/El_Alto" title="El Alto">El Alto</a> section of <a href="/wiki/La_Paz" title="La Paz">La Paz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Single-sex_education" title="Single-sex education">Single-sex education</a> has traditionally been dominant and is still highly relevant. Universal education, meaning state-provided primary and secondary education independent of gender, is not yet a global norm, even if it is assumed in most developed countries. In some Western countries, women have surpassed men at many levels of education. For example, in the United States in 2005/2006, women earned 62% of associate degrees, 58% of bachelor's degrees, 60% of master's degrees, and 50% of doctorates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>The educational <a href="/wiki/Sex_ratio" title="Sex ratio">gender gap</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development</a> (OECD) countries has been reduced over the last 30 years. Younger women today are far more likely to have completed a tertiary qualification: in 19 of the 30 OECD countries, more than twice as many women aged 25 to 34 have completed tertiary education than have women aged 55 to 64. In 21 of 27 OECD countries with comparable data, the number of women graduating from university-level programmes is equal to or exceeds that of men. 15-year-old girls tend to show much higher expectations for their careers than boys of the same age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
While women account for more than half of university graduates in several OECD countries, they receive only 30% of tertiary degrees granted in science and engineering fields, and women account for only 25% to 35% of researchers in most OECD countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research shows that while women are studying at prestigious universities at the same rate as men they are not being given the same chance to join the faculty. Sociologist <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Zuckerman" title="Harriet Zuckerman">Harriet Zuckerman</a> has observed that the more prestigious an institute is, the more difficult and time-consuming it will be for women to obtain a faculty position there. In 1989, Harvard University tenured its first woman in chemistry, Cynthia Friend, and in 1992 its first woman in physics, Melissa Franklin. She also observed that women were more likely to hold their first <a href="/wiki/Professional" title="Professional">professional</a> positions as instructors and lecturers while men are more likely to work first in tenure positions. According to Smith and Tang, as of 1989, 65% of men and only 40% of women held tenured positions and only 29% of all scientists and engineers employed as assistant professors in four-year colleges and universities were women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1992, women earned 9% of the PhDs awarded in <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>, but only one percent of those women became professors. In 1995, 11% of professors in science and engineering were women. In relation, only 311 deans of engineering schools were women, which is less than 1% of the total. Even in <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a>, a degree in which women earn the majority of PhDs, they hold a significant amount of fewer tenured positions, roughly 19% in 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literacy">Literacy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">Literacy</a></div>
